Ruling on Cutting Trees and Grass in the Haram
Ruling with proof on Haram plant compensation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on cutting four types of plants in the Haram and apply the exception for idhkhir.

Core Idea
Cutting protected Haram plants requires compensation unless dry or specifically excepted (idhkhir).

In this lesson, we examine the detailed Hanafi classification of trees and herbage in the Haram. The passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq establishes when cutting or grazing incurs compensation and when it is free. Understanding these categories is essential for any student of Hajj fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The act of cutting or grazing herbage and trees in the Haram. The general ruling is prohibition (haram), with exceptions.
The prohibition applies to both fresh and dry plants, unless dry wood or idhkhir.
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith: 'Its herbage shall not be cut, nor its thorns broken' with the exception for idhkhir. This is the primary proof.
The hadith is ahad (zanni) but accepted as evidence.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The general negative command indicates prohibition; the exception (istithna) excludes idhkhir from the prohibition. Thus idhkhir is permissible.
The exception was dictated by the Prophet based on the request of al-‘Abbas.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf allowed grazing herbage due to hardship for visitors. The majority Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa, Muhammad) rejects this because the text is explicit.
The mufta bihi is the prohibition.
5
Apply the classification
The passage divides trees and herbage into four categories based on human cultivation and natural growth. Only one category (naturally growing, non-cultivated type) requires compensation; the rest are free.
Dry wood is an additional exception: no compensation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جزاء قتل الصيد وجرحه حشيش الحرم أو شجراً غير مملوك، وهو مما لا ينبته الناس ضمن قيمته إلا فيما جف، وحرم رعي حشيش الحرم وقطعه إلا الإذخر، المراد به ههنا مطلق الكلا رطبا كان أو يابسا وفي الأصل الحشيش: ما يبس من الكلا. (فتح) كالشيح والقيصوم ونحوهما أي القاطع العضاه، حشيش الحرم ويتصدق بها ، ولا مدخل للصوم في هذا، وما عدا هذا يحل قطعه والانتفاع به بلا جزاء. (ط) المراد به ههنا مطلق الكلاً رطبا كان أو يابسا وفي الأصل الحشيش: ما يبس من ضمن قيمته: سواء كان القاطع محرماً أو حلالاً، ثم إذا أدى القيمة ملكه غير أنه يكره له بيعه والانتفاع به، أما الصيد الذي أدى جزاءه فلا يجوز بيعه؛ لأن الصيد محظور إحرامه بالنص القطعي، وأما شجر الحرم وحشيشه فلا تعلق للإحرام به والقارن في ذلك كالمفرد لا يجب عليه إلا قيمة واحدة والقلع كالقطع، ولا مدخل للصوم في هذه والحاصل أن شجر الحرم وحشيشه أربعة أنواع: ثلاثة منها يحل قطعها والانتفاع بها بلا جزاء، وواحدة منها لا يحل قطعها والانتفاع بها بدون الجزاء، أما الثلاثة الأول : فكل شجر أنبته الناس وهو من جنس ما ينبته الناس، وكل شجر أنبته الناس وهو ليس من جنس ما ينبته الناس وكل شجر نبت بنفسه وهو من جنس ما ينبته الناس وهذه يحل قطعها؛ لأن ما ينبته الناس عادة غير مستحق الأمن بالإجماع، وما لا ينبت عادة إذا أنبته الناس التحق بما ينبت عادةً، وأما الواحدة التي لا يحل قطعها فهي كل شجر نبت بنفسه وهو ليس من جنس ما ينبته الناس ولو نبت بنفسه ما لا ينبت عادة في ملك رجل بأن نبت في ملكه أم غيلان وهو نوع من بالهاء الأصلية على وزن كتاب شجر السواك ينعقد عليه الصمغ العربي، يجب على قاطعه قيمته لمالكه، وقيمته لحق الشرع كما لو قتل صيداً مملوكاً في الحرم. (فتح) إلا فيما جف أي ضمن قيمته إلا في شجر الحرم الذي يبس فإنه لا يضمن ويحل الانتفاع به؛ لأنه حطب وليس بنام ولهذا جاز أخذ الكمأة منه لعدم نموها وثبوت الحرمة بسبب الحرم لما يكون نامياً، والشجر المنكسر کالیابس والمذكور في الكتب أن قطع الكلأ مطلقاً يوجب الجزاء، والفرق بينه وبين الشجر غير ظاهر، ولو جعل الاستثناء من الحشيش والشجر كان مقتضى القياس. (فتح) إلا الإذخر: أي يحرم رعي حشيش الحرم وقطعه إلا الإذخر فإنه يجوز قطعه ورعيه، وهو ما ينبت في السهل والجبل، وله أصل دقيق وقضبان دقاق يطيب ريحه والذي بمكة أجوده يسقفون به البيوت بين الخشبات ويسدون به في القبور الخلل بين اللبنات، والأصل في ذلك أنه عليها لما قال: "لا يختلى خلاها ولا يعضد شوكها"، قال عباس الله: يا رسول الله ! إلا الإذخر ، فإنه لرعي دوابنا وقبورنا، فقال: "إلا الإذخر"، ومثله يسمى بالاستثناء التلقيني، ولهم عطف تلقيني أيضاً، ومنه قالوا: "والمقصرين" الحديث، وقال أبو يوسف: لا بأس برعي الحشيش؛ لمكان الحرج في حق الزائرين والمقيمين والحجة عليه ما روينا والقطع بالمشافر كالقطع بالمناجل، وحمل الحشيش من الحل متيسر فلا حرج ولئن كان فيه حرج فلا يعتبر؛ لأن الحرج إنما يعتبر في موضع لا نص فيه، وأما مع النص بخلافه فلا. (فتح، مستخلص)
Chapter of Hajj: Compensation for killing game and wounding. The herbage of the Haram or a tree not owned, which is not cultivated by people, its value is guaranteed except what has dried. Grazing the herbage of the Haram and cutting it are prohibited except al-idhkhir. What is meant here is absolute grass, fresh or dry. In the original, al-hashish is what has dried from grass. (Fath) Like shih and qaysum and similar [plants] meaning the cutter [؟] of the thorny trees, the herbage of the Haram, and he gives it in charity, and there is no fasting in this. What besides this, its cutting and benefiting from it are permissible without compensation. (Tah) The intended meaning here is absolute grass, fresh or dry. In the original, al-hashish: what has dried from grass. Its value is guaranteed: whether the cutter is a muhrim or non-muhrim. Then when he pays the value, he owns it, but it is disliked for him to sell it and benefit from it. As for game for which he paid compensation, its sale is not permissible because game is prohibited by the sacred state of ihram by definitive text. As for the trees and herbage of the Haram, ihram has no connection to them. The qarin in this is like the mufrid; only one value is required. Uprooting is like cutting. There is no fasting in these. The summary: the trees and herbage of the Haram are four types: three of them, their cutting and benefiting from them are permissible without compensation, and one of them, its cutting and benefiting are not permissible without compensation. As for the three: every tree that people have cultivated and is of the type that people cultivate, every tree that people have cultivated but is not of the type that people cultivate, and every tree that grows by itself and is of the type that people cultivate—these, their cutting is permissible because what people usually cultivate is not entitled to security by consensus, and what is not usually cultivated, when people cultivate it, it becomes like what is usually cultivated. As for the one whose cutting is not permissible, it is every tree that grows by itself and is not of the type that people cultivate. Even if what does not usually grow by itself grows in a man's property, such as umm ghaylan, a type with the original ha', on the weight of kitab, a toothbrush tree, from which gum arabic is formed—the cutter must pay its value to its owner, and its value is due to the right of the Law, as if he killed owned game in the Haram. (Fath) Except what has dried: i.e., its value is guaranteed except for the tree of the Haram that has dried, for it is not guaranteed and its benefit is permissible because it is firewood, not a plant. Therefore, taking truffles from it is permissible due to lack of growth, and the prohibition due to the sanctuary applies to what is growing. Broken trees are like dry ones. It is mentioned in the books that cutting grass absolutely requires compensation, and the difference between it and trees is not apparent. If the exception were made from herbage and trees, it would be in accordance with analogy. (Fath) Except al-idhkhir: i.e., grazing and cutting the herbage of the Haram are prohibited except al-idhkhir, for its cutting and grazing are permissible. It is what grows in the plain and mountain, with a slender root and slender branches, fragrant, and the best is in Makkah. They roof houses with it between beams and fill cracks in graves between bricks. The basis for this is that he (peace be upon him) said: 'Its herbage shall not be cut, nor its thorns broken.' Al-‘Abbas (may Allah be pleased with him) said: 'O Messenger of Allah, except al-idhkhir, for it is for our beasts and our graves.' He said: 'Except al-idhkhir.' Such is called dictated exception. They also have dictated conjunction. From it they said: 'And those who shorten.' The hadith. Abu Yusuf said: 'There is no harm in grazing herbage due to hardship for visitors and residents.' The proof against him is what we narrated. Cutting with the mouth is like cutting with scythes. Carrying herbage from the hill (outside the Haram) is easy, so no hardship. Even if there were hardship, it is not considered because hardship is only considered where there is no text, but with a text to the contrary, not. (Fath, Mukhtasar)
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling (ruling_with_proof) on the compensation for cutting or harming plants in the Haram. It begins by stating that any herbage or tree not owned, which is not cultivated by people, requires compensation for its value, except for dry plants. The grazing and cutting of herbage are generally prohibited, with the exception of idhkhir. The text clarifies that 'hashish' here means all grass, fresh or dry. Compensation is due whether the cutter is in ihram or not. After paying the value, the cutter owns the plant but it is disliked to sell it, unlike game for which sale is absolutely impermissible because of the definitive prohibition of ihram. The passage then categorizes trees and herbage into four types: three are permissible to cut without compensation: (1) trees cultivated by people of a type they cultivate, (2) trees cultivated by people of a type not usually cultivated, (3) trees growing naturally but of a type that people cultivate. The fourth type, which requires compensation, is trees growing naturally and not of a type people cultivate. An example is the 'um ghaylan' tree, which produces gum arabic. The dry wood is an exception; it is like firewood and not considered growing plants, so no compensation. The exception of idhkhir is based on the hadith where the Prophet allowed it for practical needs. Abu Yusuf's opinion permitting grazing due to hardship is refuted by the explicit text. The passage also notes that the qarin only pays one value, not two, because the prohibition is on the place, not the state of ihram.

Key Terms
حشيش (hashish): Here means all grass, fresh or dry, of the Haram.
إذخر (idhkhir): A fragrant plant used for roofing and filling graves, excepted from the prohibition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on cutting a tree that grows naturally in the Haram and is not of a type that people cultivate?
A) Permissible without compensation.
B) Permissible only if dry.
C) Requires compensation for its value.
D) Prohibited absolutely.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, cutting protected plants of the Haram is prohibited, requiring compensation, except for dry wood and idhkhir.

The Qarin Must Offer Two Dams: The Hanafi Position
Ruling that a qarin owes two dams for each prohibition, with an exception for the miqat case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school requires two dams from a qarin for violating ihram prohibitions, and identify the exception for passing the miqat without ihram.

Core Idea
A qarin violates two ihrams, so he must pay two dams — except when he bypasses the miqat without ihram.

This passage addresses a key disagreement between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the number of compensatory sacrifices (dam) required from a pilgrim performing Hajj and Umrah together (qarin). The Hanafi position is that he owes two dams because he is in two distinct states of ihram, while the Shafi'i school considers ihram a single state. The text also discusses an exception for those who pass the miqat without ihram and an intra-Hanafi difference with Imam Zafar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi ruling and its basis
The qarin must pay two dams for any prohibited act that would require one dam from a mufrid. The basis is that the qarin has two ihrams—one for Hajj and one for Umrah.
The text says 'on the qarin two dams' for each prohibition.
2
Note the exception for the miqat
If the qarin passes the miqat without ihram and later enters ihram inside the miqat, only one dam is required. Imam Zafar disagreed, but the majority holds one dam.
The exception only applies to this specific scenario, not to other violations.
3
Examine the opposing view
Al-Shafi'i holds that one dam is sufficient because the qarin has only one ihram, based on the principle of overlapping (tadakhul) of intentions.
The Shafi'i school does not dualize the ihram.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi argument is that the qarin has two distinct ihrams, and each was violated, so two compensations are necessary.
This contrasts with the Shafi'i view that the intentions merge into one ihram.
5
Consider the intra-Hanafi disagreement
Imam Zafar argued that even in the miqat case two dams are due, but the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is one dam because the obligation was only one ihram at the miqat.
The majority Hanafi view prevails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكل شيء على المفرد به دم فعلى القارن دمان، إلا أن يجاوز الميقات غير محرم. وكل شيء من الأشياء التي أمر باجتنابها (ط) فعلى القارن :دمان [دم لحجته ودم لعمرته، ويلحق به المتمتع الذي ساق الهدي. (ط)] يعني على القارن بفعل شيء من محظورات إحرامه دمان أو صدقتان لا مطلقا؛ إذ لو ترك واجبا من واجبات الحج أو قطع نبات الحرم لم يتعدد الجزاء؛ لأنه ليس جناية على الإحرام، وقال الشافعي: على القارن دم واحد بناء على أنه محرم بإحرام واحد عنده؛ لأنه يقول بالتداخل وعندنا: محرم بإحرامين وقد جنى عليهما، فيجب عليه دمان وذكر شيخ الإسلام أن وجوب الدمين على القارن فيما إذا كان قبل الوقوف بعرفة، وأما بعد الوقوف ففي الجماع يجب دمان، وفي غيره من المحظورات دم واحد. (فتح) إلا أن يجاوز: أي على القارن دمان في كل صورة يجب على المفرد فيها دم إلا في صورة واحدة، وهي صورة مجاوزة الميقات بلا إحرام، ثم أحرم بعد المجاوزة بالحج والعمرة داخل الميقات فيلزمه دم واحد عندنا، وقال زفر: يلزمه دمان؛ لأنه أخر الإحرامين من الميقات، فيلزمه لكل واحد منهما دم اعتباراً بسائر المحظورات، ولنا: أن الواجب عليه إحرام واحد لأجل تعظيم البقعة، ولهذا لو أحرم من الميقات بالعمرة وأحرم بالحج داخل الميقات، لا يجب عليه شيء وهو قارن وبترك واجب واحد لا يجب إلا جزاء واحد؛ لأن الواجب عليه عند دخول الميقات أحد النسكين، فإذا جاوزه بغير إحرام ثم أحرم بها، فقد أدخل النقص على ما هو المستحق عليه، وهو أحدهما فلزمه جزاء واحد. (فتح) غير محرم فإنه حينئذ يجب عليه دم واحد. (ط،ع)
And everything for which the mufrid owes one dam, the qarin owes two dams, except if he passes the miqat without being in ihram. And everything from the things that are commanded to be avoided (Tah), then on the qarin two dams [one dam for his Hajj and one dam for his Umrah, and the mutamatti' who sent a hadyi is included with him. (Tah)] That means: on the qarin, for doing something of the prohibitions of his ihram, two dams or two charities, not absolutely; because if he left a wajib of the obligations of Hajj or cut a plant of the Haram, the compensation is not multiplied because it is not an offense against ihram. Al-Shafi'i said: On the qarin one dam, based on his view that he is in one ihram, because he believes in overlapping (tadakhul). According to us, he is in two ihrams and has offended against both, so two dams are required. Shaykh al-Islam mentioned that the obligation of two dams on the qarin is only before standing at 'Arafah; after standing, for sexual intercourse two dams are required, but for other prohibitions one dam. (Fath) Except that he passes: i.e., on the qarin two dams in every case where the mufrid owes one dam, except in one case: the case of passing the miqat without ihram, then after passing he enters ihram for Hajj and Umrah inside the miqat, then one dam is incumbent on him according to us. Zafar said: Two dams are incumbent because he delayed both ihrams from the miqat, so each requires a dam by analogy to other prohibitions. Our argument: what is obligatory on him is one ihram for the purpose of revering the place; therefore, if he entered ihram for Umrah from the miqat and then entered ihram for Hajj inside the miqat, nothing is obligatory on him, and he is a qarin. And by leaving one wajib, only one compensation is required because what was obligatory on him upon entering the miqat was one of the two rituals. So when he passed it without ihram and then entered ihram, he has introduced deficiency upon what was due on him, which is one of them, so one compensation is required. (Fath) Not being in ihram: in that case one dam is required. (Tah, 'Ayni)
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute about the number of dam required from a qarin when he violates a prohibition of ihram. The Hanafi position is that he owes two dams because he is in two ihrams (one for Hajj and one for Umrah). This is in contrast to Imam al-Shafi'i, who considers the qarin to be in a single ihram and thus only one dam. The text notes an exception: if a qarin passes the miqat without ihram and then enters ihram inside the miqat, he owes only one dam. Imam Zafar disagreed, but the majority Hanafi view holds that only one dam is due because the obligation was to enter ihram at the miqat for one of the two rituals, so only one compensation is required. The passage also clarifies that this multiplication only applies to prohibitions that are offenses against ihram, not to leaving wajibat or cutting plants.

Key Terms
قارن (qarin): One who performs Hajj and Umrah together with the same ihram.
دم (dam): The compulsory sacrifice required for violating ihram prohibitions.
تداخل (tadakhul): The principle that multiple intentions merge into a single legal consequence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is true about a qarin who passes the miqat without ihram and later enters ihram inside?
A) He owes two dams for any subsequent prohibition.
B) He owes one dam for any subsequent prohibition.
C) He owes no dam because he has not committed a violation.
D) He must slaughter a cow.

Key Takeaway
A qarin must pay two dams for ihram violations because he has two ihrams, except when he failed to enter ihram at the miqat, then he pays one dam.

Multiple Muhrims Killing Game: Each Owes Full Compensation
Ruling on compensation for game killed by multiple muhrim, with Shafi'i dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that each muhirim owes full compensation for jointly killing game and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
When two muhrim jointly kill game, each owes a full jaza' — the violation is against individual ihram, not the single place.

This lesson examines a classic inter-madhhab dispute: when two pilgrims in ihram jointly kill a game, do they owe one compensation between them or each a full compensation? The Hanafi school rules that each must pay a complete fidyah, while the Shafi'i school holds that a single compensation suffices. We will explore the proofs and reasoning behind each position, with special attention to the dual nature of jaza' as both expiation and replacement.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Begin by identifying the exact ruling: for two muhrim jointly killing game, each owes a full compensation (jaza' kamil). This is the established Hanafi position.
The term 'taddad al-jaza'' in the matn indicates multiplication.
2
Identify the Shafi'i objection
Next, recognize that Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, arguing that only one compensation is due because the obligation is a pure replacement (badal) for the value of the game, which is a single unit.
The Shafi'i proof relies on the analogy of halal people in the Haram and the principle of damage compensation.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
Examine the Hanafi counter-argument: jaza' combines expiation (kaffarah) and replacement (badal). Since it includes expiation, and expiation is due for each individual's offense, it multiplies with each muhrim. This is supported by the Quranic term 'jaza'' and the fact that fasting can fulfill it.
The key is that the prohibition is on the state of ihram, which is individual, not on the place.
4
Distinguish the case of halal people
Contrast with two halal people killing game in the Haram: here, the prohibition attaches to the single place (the Haram), so only one compensation is due. This distinction clarifies the underlying rationale.
Analogous to two killers of one person: one diyah combined, but each owes a kaffarah.
5
Determine the practical consequence
Finally, note the thamarah al-khilaf: in Hanafi practice, each muhrim pays a full fidyah; in Shafi'i practice, they share one. This affects how pilgrims and guides advise in such situations.
The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is the position of each paying separately.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قتل محرمان صيداً تعدد الجزاء، ولو حلالان لا. تعدد الجزاء [ أي على كل واحد منهما جزاء كامل (ط)] وقال الشافعي عليهما جزاء واحد؛ لأن ما يجب بقتل الصيد بدل محض ألا ترى أنه يزداد الواجب بكبره وينقص بصغره، ولو كان كفارة لما اختلف باختلاف المتلف ككفارة القتل لا تختلف باختلاف قيمة العبد المقتول، فصار كحلالين اشتركا في قتل صيد الحرم، ولنا: أن هذا كفارة أي جزاء الجناية وبدل المحل؛ لأنه تعالى سماه جزاء بقوله: ﴿ فَجَزَاءٌ مِثْلُ مَا قَتَلَ مِنَ النَّعَمِ ﴾ (المائدة: ٩٥)، فجمعنا بين الأمرين عملاً بالدليلين. [ تبيين الحقائق: ۳۹۲/۲] بخلاف الحلالين؛ ولأن المحرم في المحرمين الإحرام وهو متعدد وفي الحلالين الحرم وهو واحد. (عيني، فتح) ولو حلالان :لا [ أي اشترك حلالان في قتل صيد الحرم (ع)] أي لا يتعدد الجزاء لو اشترك حلالان في قتل صيد الحرم؛ لأن الواجب فيه بدل المحل، لا جزاء الجناية، حتى لا مدخل للصوم فيه فلا يتعدد إلا بتعدد المحل، کرجلين قتلا رجلاً خطأ يجب عليهما دية واحدة؛ لأنها بدل المحل، وعلى كل واحد منهما كفارة؛ لأنها جزاء الفعل بخلاف المحرمين؛ لأن الواجب هناك جزاء الجناية، ولهذا يتأدى بالصوم. (فتح)
And if two muhrim kill game, the compensation is multiplied; if two halal people [kill game in the Haram], it is not. The compensation is multiplied [meaning: on each of them a full compensation (Tah)]. Al-Shafi'i said: On both a single compensation, because what is obligatory for killing game is a pure replacement; do you not see that the obligation increases with its size and decreases with its smaller size? If it were an expiation, it would not vary with the variation of the object destroyed, like the expiation for killing does not vary with the value of the killed slave. So it becomes like two halal people who jointly kill a game in the Haram. Our argument: This is an expiation, i.e., a compensation for the offense and a replacement for the place; because Allah called it 'jaza'' in His saying: 'Then a compensation like what he killed from the livestock' (5:95), so we combined both aspects by acting on both proofs. [Tabyin al-Haqa'iq 2/392] Unlike the two halal people; because in the case of two muhrim, the prohibition is on the state of ihram, which is multiple, whereas in the case of two halal people, the prohibition is on the sanctuary, which is one. ('Ayni, Fath) And if two halal people: no [meaning: two halal people jointly kill a game in the Haram ('Ayni)], i.e., the compensation is not multiplied if two halal people jointly kill a game in the Haram, because what is obligatory is a replacement for the place, not a compensation for the offense. Therefore, fasting does not enter into it, so it is not multiplied except by multiplicity of the place, like two men who kill a man by mistake: one diyah is due on them because it is a replacement for the place, and on each of them a kaffarah because it is compensation for the act. Contrary to two muhrim, because there the obligatory is compensation for the offense, and therefore it can be fulfilled by fasting. (Fath)
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute on whether two muhrim who jointly kill game owe one or two compensations. The Hanafi position is that each muhrim owes a full compensation because the violation is against the state of ihram, which is individual and multiple. In contrast, Imam al-Shafi'i argues that it is a single compensation because the obligation is a replacement for the value of the game, which is one. The Hanafi response is that the term 'jaza'' in the Quran indicates both expiation (kaffarah) and replacement, so they treat it as a violation requiring expiation, which is not divisible. For two halal people (non-muhrim) killing game in the Haram, they owe only one compensation because the prohibition attaches to the place, which is one. This is analogous to two people killing a man by mistake: they owe one diyah (blood money) as compensation for the lost life, but each also owes a kaffarah (expiation) for the act of killing.

Key Terms
جزاء (jaza'): Compensation for killing game in the Haram or while in ihram, combining expiation and replacement.
بدل المحل (badal al-mahal): Replacement for the value of the place or thing, not for the act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Two halal people (non-muhrim) jointly kill a game in the Haram. How many compensations are required according to the Hanafi school?
A) One compensation between them.
B) Two compensations, one each.
C) No compensation because they are halal.
D) Each must pay expiation.

Key Takeaway
When multiple muhrim kill game, each owes a full compensation because the violation is against each individual's state of ihram.

Invalidity of a Muhrim's Sale of Game
Ruling that a muhrim's sale of game is void

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that a muhrim's sale of game is batil and explain the two reasons for its invalidity.

Core Idea
A muhrim cannot sell game: if alive, prohibited to hunt; if dead, it is carrion.

This lesson examines a clear ruling from the Hanafi school: the sale of game by a person in ihram (muhrim) is absolutely void. The reasoning is twofold and relates to the protected status of game and the muhrim's legal capacity. Understanding this ruling reinforces the broader principle that the state of ihram restrictions extends to transactions involving game.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the sale (bay') of game (sayd) by a person in ihram (muhrim). The ruling is that the sale is void (batil).
This is a legal invalidity, not merely prohibited; the contract has no effect.
2
Analyze the two scenarios
The ruling covers two possibilities: (1) selling the game while it is alive, and (2) selling it after killing it. Both are invalid but for different reasons.
3
Understand the legal reasoning for each
For the alive scenario: the muhrim is prohibited from hunting protected game (sayd amin), so dealing with it is impermissible. For the dead scenario: the muhrim cannot slaughter validly, so the animal becomes carrion (maytah), which is najis and unsalable.
The Lawgiver removed the muhrim's capacity (ahliyyah) for slaughter, making the meat carrion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبطل بيع المحرم صيداً وجه البطلان؛ لأنه إن باعه حياً فقد تعرض للصيد الآمن وهو منهي عنه، وإن باعه بعد ما قتله فقد باع ميتة؛ لأن الشارع أخرجه عن أهلية الذبح، وأطلق المصنف فأفاد أن بيع المحرم باطل ولو كان المشتري =
And the sale of game by a muhrim is void, and the reason for its invalidity: because if he sells it alive, he has exposed the protected game which is prohibited to him, and if he sells it after killing it, he has sold a carcass, because the Lawgiver has removed it from the capacity of slaughter. The author has indicated that the sale of a muhrim is void even if the buyer is [a non-muhrim?]
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage is a clear ruling statement that a muhrim's sale of game is void. The reasoning is twofold. If the muhrim sells the game while it is alive, he is dealing with a 'protected' game (sayd amin), which is prohibited to him because of his ihram. If he sells it after he has killed it, it is considered a carcass (maytah), because the Lawgiver has removed the muhrim's capacity to slaughter validly. The author (the matn writer) indicates that the invalidity is absolute, even if the buyer is a non-muhrim or the sale occurs outside the Haram. The text ends mid-sentence, but the ruling is clear.

Key Terms
ميتة (maytah): Carrion; an animal that died without proper Islamic slaughter.
آمن (amin): Protected; game in the Haram or while in ihram is considered safe from harm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A muhrim kills a deer and wants to sell its meat. What is the ruling according to the Hanafi school?
A) The sale is valid because the deer was killed.
B) The sale is void because the muhrim cannot slaughter validly.
C) The sale is valid if the buyer is halal.
D) The sale is makruh but valid.

Key Takeaway
A muhrim's sale of game is always batil, whether the game is sold alive (prohibited) or dead (carrion).

Joint Killing of Game by Muhrims: Multiple Penalties
Ruling on joint killing of game by muhrims and halals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that each muhrim jointly killing game owes a full penalty and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Each muhrim’s ihram is an independent cause, so joint killing triggers multiple penalties.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq and its commentary addresses a dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools over joint killing of game by muhrims. The key question is whether the penalty multiples with the number of killers. Understanding the underlying cause—ihram versus location—is essential to grasp the Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Note the ruling for muhrims (penalty multiplies) and for halals (does not multiply). This contrast highlights the operative cause.
The passage opens with the rule.
2
Examine the Shafi'i objection
Imam al-Shafi'i argues killing game is pure compensation (badal mahd), so joint killers share one penalty, like two halals.
Shafi'i uses analogy with diyah.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The Quran (5:95) calls it 'jaza', which in Hanafi usul implies both compensation and expiation. The dual nature allows multiplication when the cause (ihram) is multiple.
Quranic dalil is the primary evidence.
4
Understand the analogy with homicide
Two men killing one man share one diyah (compensation) but each owes a kaffarah (expiation). Similarly, joint muhrims each owe a full penalty because it includes expiation.
This supports the dual nature.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practically, Hanafi: two dams; Shafi'i: one dam. This difference flows from the nature of the penalty.
The practical consequence is the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(فتح)
And if two muhrims kill game, the penalty multiplies, and if two halals (non-muhrims) [kill], it does not. [Commentary: i.e., each one owes a complete penalty (T)] And al-Shafi'i said: upon them both is one penalty; because what is obligatory for killing game is pure compensation (badal). Do you not see that the obligation increases with its size and decreases with its smallness? If it were an expiation (kaffarah), it would not vary with the value of the object, like the expiation for killing does not vary with the value of the slave killed. So it becomes like two halals who jointly kill game of the Haram. And our evidence: that this is an expiation (kaffarah), i.e., a penalty for the transgression and compensation for the value (badal al-mahal); because Allah Most High called it 'jaza' in His saying: 'So the penalty is the like of what he killed from the cattle' (al-Ma'idah 5:95), so we combine between the two matters, acting upon both proofs. (from Tabiyin al-Haqa'iq 2/392) Unlike the two halals. And because for the two muhrims, the prohibitory cause is ihram which is multiple, and for the two halals it is the Haram which is one. (from 'Ayni, Fath) And if two halals: i.e., if two halals jointly kill game of the Haram, the penalty does not multiply; because what is obligatory is compensation for the value (badal al-mahal), not a penalty for a transgression, and therefore fasting has no place in it; so it does not multiply except with multiple objects, like two men who kill a man by mistake: they owe one blood money (diyah) because it is compensation for the value, and each one owes an expiation (kaffarah) because that is a penalty for the action. Unlike the two muhrims, because what is obligatory there is a penalty for a transgression, and for this reason it can be fulfilled by fasting. (from Fath)

Commentary
This passage deals with the penalty when two people jointly kill game. The rule distinguishes between the killers being muhrims (in ihram) or halals (non-muhrims). If two muhrims jointly kill game, each owes a full penalty (dam) because each has violated his ihram. If two halals jointly kill game in the Haram, they owe only one penalty between them because the prohibition is attached to the location (Haram), which is one. The passage then records a dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i, who argues that killing game is pure compensation (badal) not expiation, and thus joint killers should share one penalty by analogy with two halals. The Hanafi response is to cite the Quranic verse calling it 'jaza' and to argue that it is both compensation and expiation; therefore, when the cause (ihram) is multiple, the penalty multiples. The Hanafi position is further supported by the analogy of two men who kill a man by mistake—they share one diyah (compensation) but each owes a kaffarah (expiation) for the act of killing. Similarly, for muhrims, the penalty combines both aspects.

Key Terms
جزاء (jazā'): Penalty for killing game; combines compensation and expiation.
بدل محض (badal maḥḍ): Pure compensation, not expiatory.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One dam
B) Two dams (one each)
C) Three dams
D) No dam

Key Takeaway
When multiple muhrims jointly kill game, each owes a full penalty because each has violated his own ihram.

Sale of Game by a Muhrim: Invalid Transaction (Batil)
Ruling with proof on sale of game by a muhrim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling (batil) for a muhrim's sale of game and explain the two reasons for invalidity: protection of live game and carrion status of dead game.

Core Idea
A muhrim's sale of game is invalid: live game is protected, dead game is carrion.

This passage from Fath al-Qadir explains that a person in the state of ihram (muhrim) cannot validly sell game (sayd), whether alive or dead. The ruling is batil (void). The commentator provides the legal reasoning (ta'lil) for this prohibition: selling live game violates the protection afforded to game while in ihram, and selling dead game amounts to selling carrion (maytah), which is itself impermissible. Moreover, the invalidity extends to any disposition involving game by a muhrim, including purchase, gift, or charity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a muhrim selling game. The ruling is batil (invalid).
2
Distinguish the two scenarios
If the game is sold live, the muhrim is tampering with protected game (sayd amin). If dead, it is carrion (maytah) because the muhrim lacks slaughter capacity.
The Lawgiver removed the muhrim's capacity for slaughter, so his killing results in a carcass that is maytah.
3
Understand the basis for invalidity
The object of sale is not legally saleable: live game is protected and cannot be transacted, dead game is maytah and cannot be sold.
4
Generalize the principle
The ruling applies to all dispositions (sale, purchase, gift, charity) by a muhrim involving game, regardless of the other party's ihram status.
The passage explicitly states that purchase is also invalid even if the seller is halal.
5
Note the penalty (jaza)
The penalty for killing game is only on the muhrim, not on a halal party involved in the transaction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبطل بيع المحرم صيداً وجه البطلان؛ لأنه إن باعه حياً فقد تعرض للصيد الآمن وهو منهي عنه، وإن باعه بعد ما قتله فقد باع ميتة؛ لأن الشارع أخرجه عن أهلية الذبح، وأطلق المصنف فأفاد أن بيع المحرم باطل ولو كان المشتري = وشراؤه. حلالاً وأن شراؤه باطل ولو كان البائع حلالاً، وأما الجزاء فإنما يكون على المحرم، وعلى هذا كل تصرف سواء كان بيعاً أو هبة أو صدقة. (فتح)
And the sale of game by a muhrim is invalid. The reason for invalidity: because if he sells it alive, he has tampered with safe game, which is prohibited; and if he sells it after killing it, he has sold carrion, because the Lawgiver has removed him from the capacity of slaughter. And the author (al-Misri) was absolute, indicating that the sale of a muhrim is invalid even if the buyer is a halal (non-muhrim). And also his purchase is invalid even if the seller is a halal. As for the penalty (jaza'), it is only upon the muhrim. And according to this, every disposition, whether sale, gift, or charity, [is invalid].
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage states the ruling that a muhrim's sale of game is invalid (batil). The reasoning is twofold: if the game is sold alive, the muhrim is exposing safe game (sayd amin) to danger, which is prohibited. If the game is already dead, the muhrim cannot slaughter it validly (since he is in ihram, he is invalid for slaughter), so the carcass becomes carrion (maytah), which cannot be sold. The author also clarifies that this invalidity applies whether the buyer is a muhrim or not, and also if the muhrim is the buyer. Furthermore, all types of dispositions (sale, gift, charity) are invalid if they involve game while in ihram. The penalty (jaza) for killing game is only incurred by the muhrim, not by a halal person involved in the transaction.

Key Terms
صيد آمن (ṣayd āmin): Game that is safe/protected; in ihram, the muhrim must not disturb it.
ميتة (maytah): Carrion; an animal not slaughtered properly, thus unfit for sale.

C) The sale is valid only if the deer was killed first

Key Takeaway

Guarantee for Mother and Offspring of Haram Gazelle
Ruling on guarantee when female gazelle removed from Haram gives birth and dies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on guarantee for a gazelle removed from the Haram and apply the qiyas reasoning that extends liability to its offspring.

Core Idea
Removing a gazelle from the Haram makes you liable for both mother and offspring—the inviolability attribute passes to the young.

This lesson examines a specific case from Kanz al-Daqa’iq: what happens if you take a female gazelle out of the Haram and she gives birth? The classical Hanafi ruling uses qiyas on 'stable attributes' (awṣāf qārrah) to extend the guarantee to the offspring. We will walk through the ruling, the proof, and the distinction from misappropriation (ghasb).

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling and its conditions
First, note the basic rule: whoever removes a female gazelle from the Haram must return it to its place of safety. If she gives birth outside and both die, the taker guarantees both. This is before any penalty payment.
The obligation to return is wajib; failure leads to dam (penalty).
2
Recognize the qiyas structure
The ruling extends to the offspring via qiyas. The asl (original case) is the mother gazelle; the hukm is guarantee due to removal from safety. The far' (new case) is the offspring born after removal. The illa (effective cause) is the 'stable attribute' of inviolability (ḥurmah) that passes from mother to offspring, like freedom or kitābah.
The illa is derived from the Shari'ah attribute of 'must be returned to Haram'.
3
Understand the exception after penalty payment
If the taker pays the penalty (jaza') for the mother before the offspring dies, then the offspring is not guaranteed. This is because the penalty expiates the violation, and the offspring inherits the mother's freed status.
After payment, the mother is no longer 'madhmun' (guaranteed), so the offspring is also free.
4
Distinguish from ghasb (misappropriation)
An objection: in ghasb, if a usurper takes a gazelle and she gives birth, the offspring is not guaranteed. The answer: in the Haram case, the right is of Allah, who always demands return; in ghasb, the right is of a human who may waive it. Also, the cause in Haram is removal of safety (amn), which extends to the offspring via the stable attribute; in ghasb, the cause is removal of the owner's hand, which does not extend to offspring born in the usurper's possession.
This distinction shows the specificity of the Haram ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن أخرج ظبية الحرم فولدت ماتا ضمنهما، فإن أدى جزاءها فولدت لا يضمن الولد. لأنه صيد الحل أي الأم والولد أي الأم بعد الأداء. ظبية الحرم من الحرم وجب عليه الرد والإرسال (مسكين فولدت أي فإن لم يفعل وولدت بعد الإرسال خارج الحرم. (مسكين) ضمنهما : أي الولد والأم؛ لأن الصيد بعد الإخراج من الحرم واجب الرد إلى موضع أمانه وهو الحرم، وهذه الصفة شرعية، فتسري إلى الولد أي يثبت وجوب الرد إلى الحرم في الأولاد أيضاً؛ لأن الأوصاف القارة في الأمهات تسري إلى الأولاد كالحرية والكتابة والتدبير فإن قيل: ضمان الولد هنا يشكل بما لو غصب ظبية فولدت فماتا حيث لا يضمن الولد. يجاب؛ لأن الولد في ظبية الحرم حق الله تعالى وهو الطالب للرد بخلافه في الغصب؛ إذ هو حق العبد ولم يطلب، حتى لو طلبه فمنعه الغاصب يضمن، وبأن السبب للضمان في ظبية الحرم إزالة الأمن وقد وجد في الولد بالسراية، وفي الغصب إزالة يد المالك ولم يوجد ؛ لأن الولد حدث عند غاصب أمه فعلى هذا يضمن ولد الظبية كيف ما كان تمكن مخرج أمه من الرد أو لم يتمكن. (عيني، فتح) لا يضمن الولد لأنه صيد حل وقد انعدم أثر فعله بالتكفير؛ لأنه حين أدى جزاء الأم بقيت غير مضمونة، فكذا الأولاد، ولهذا يملكها الذي أخرجها أي ملكاً خبيثاً، وكذا إن زادت بعد الإخراج في البدن أو السعر فولدت وماتا ضمنهما، ولو بعد أداء الجزاء لا يضمنهما، وكذا إن باعها فزادت أو ولدت في يد المشتري ثم ماتا ضمنهما البائع قبل التكفير لا بعده. (عيني، مسكين)
And whoever takes out a female gazelle from the Haram, and she gives birth and both die, he guarantees them both. If he pays her penalty (jaza') and then she gives birth, he does not guarantee the offspring. Because the offspring is game of the Hal (outside Haram). Meaning the mother and the offspring, i.e., the mother after payment. A female gazelle from the Haram: upon leaving it, it is obligatory to return and release it (it is said: if he does not do so and she gives birth after removal outside the Haram). He guarantees both: i.e., the offspring and the mother; because game after being taken out of the Haram is obligatory to be returned to its place of safety, which is the Haram, and this attribute (hukm) is shari'ah, so it extends to the offspring, i.e., the obligation to return to the Haram is established for the offspring as well; because stable attributes in mothers extend to offspring, like freedom, kitabah, and tadbir. If it is said: the guarantee of the offspring here is problematic compared to when someone misappropriates (ghasb) a gazelle and she gives birth and both die, where the offspring is not guaranteed. The answer: because in the case of the Haram gazelle, the right is of Allah, and He demands the return; unlike misappropriation where it is the right of the servant and he has not demanded [return]—indeed, if he demands it and the usurper prevents, then he guarantees. And also because the cause for guarantee in the Haram gazelle is the removal of safety (amn), which exists in the offspring through extension; while in misappropriation, it is the removal of the owner's hand, which does not exist because the offspring came into existence with the usurper of the mother. Thus, one guarantees the offspring of the gazelle regardless of whether the remover of the mother was able to return [to the Haram] or not. (from 'Ayni, Fath) And he does not guarantee the offspring because it is game of the Hal (outside Haram), and the effect of his action has been nullified by the expiation (takfir); because when he paid the penalty for the mother, she remains unguaranteed, and so too the offspring. And for this reason, the one who took it out owns it (ownership in a vile sense). Likewise, if after removal there was an increase in body or price, and then she gives birth and both die, he guarantees them; but if after paying the penalty he does not guarantee them. Similarly, if he sells her and she increases or gives birth in the buyer's hand, and then both die, the seller guarantees before expiation, not after. (from 'Ayni, Miskin)
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage covers the complex case of removing a female gazelle from the Haram. The rule: whoever takes a gazelle out of the Haram must return it. If the gazelle gives birth outside the Haram and both die, the person is liable (guarantees) for both mother and offspring. The liability for the offspring arises because the shari'ah attribute of 'must be returned to the Haram' extends to the offspring by analogy with other attributes like freedom or kitabah. However, if the person pays the penalty (jaza/dam) for the mother, then if she later gives birth and dies, he does not guarantee the offspring—because the penalty removes the liability for the mother, and the offspring inherits that exemption. The passage also distinguishes from ghasb (misappropriation): in ghasb, the offspring is not guaranteed because the right is of a human who may not demand return, and the cause is removal of the owner's hand which does not extend to the offspring. The passage further discusses increases in body or price.

Key Terms
ظبية الحرم (ẓabyat al-ḥaram): A female gazelle that is within the boundaries of the Haram (Mecca).
أوصاف قارة (awṣāf qārrah): Stable/permanent attributes that pass from mother to offspring (e.g., freedom, status as kitabah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One penalty (for the mother)
B) Two penalties (mother and offspring)
C) No penalty
D) Only the offspring's penalty

Key Takeaway
A gazelle removed from the Haram incurs liability for both mother and offspring until the mother's penalty is paid, due to the extension of stable attributes.

Liability for a Female Gazelle of the Haram and Her Offspring
Ruling on liability for a gazelle of the Haram and its offspring when taken out

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for a female gazelle taken from the Haram and her offspring, and distinguish it from the case of usurpation based on the nature of the right (haqq Allah vs. haqq al-‘abd).

Core Idea
Taking a Haram gazelle makes you liable for her offspring until you pay the penalty — the right of Allah extends to the young.

This passage addresses a nuanced fiqh issue: when a female gazelle is taken from the Haram and gives birth, who is liable for the offspring? The ruling hinges on whether the taker has already atoned for the mother. The text also defends the Hanafi position against an apparent contradiction with the law of usurpation (ghasp), revealing a deeper principle about the difference between divine rights and human rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that taking a female gazelle from the Haram triggers an obligation to return it and, if it dies, to pay the penalty (jaza'). The same applies to her offspring if they die before the mother's atonement.
The liability is wajib, not fard, because the evidence is zanni.
2
Determine the temporal scope of liability for offspring
If the taker pays the penalty for the mother and then she gives birth, the offspring is considered game of the Halal and no penalty is due. Before atonement, the offspring inherits the mother's attribute of being subject to return.
This is the key practical distinction.
3
Trace the reasoning: sariyat al-wasf
The attribute of being required to return to the Haram (wasf) is a legal attribute that extends from the mother to the offspring, similar to how freedom or kitabah extends. Thus, the offspring is initially bound by the same obligation.
This principle is used to justify liability before atonement.
4
Consider the objection from usurpation
An opponent might analogize to ghasp: if a person usurps a female gazelle and she gives birth, the usurper is not liable for the offspring. How is the Haram case different? The text answers by distinguishing the right involved — haqq Allah vs. haqq al-‘abd — and the cause of liability.
The cause in Haram is removal of security (amān), which extends to offspring; in ghasp it is removal of the owner's hand, which does not.
5
Apply to variants
The same rule applies to other dispositions (sale, gift) and to increases in value or body. The seller is liable for any offspring born in the buyer's hand before atonement, but not after.
This shows consistency across scenarios.

(عيني، مسكين)
Penalty for killing game and wounding it, and buying it. And whoever takes out a female gazelle of the Haram and she gives birth, [if] they die, he is liable for both. If he pays its penalty, and then she gives birth, [the offspring] is game of the Halal; he is not liable for the offspring. Because it is game of the Halal - that is, the mother and the offspring - that is, the mother after payment is lawful, and its purchase is invalid even if the seller was Halal. As for the penalty, it is only upon the Muhrim. And upon this [rule] is every disposition, whether sale, gift, or charity. (Fath) [Regarding] a female gazelle of the Haram from the Haram, it is obligatory to return and release it. (Miskin) Then she gave birth - that is, if he did not do so and she gave birth after release outside the Haram. (Miskin) He is liable for both: the offspring and the mother; because game after being taken out of the Haram is obligatory to return to its place of safety, which is the Haram. This attribute is a legal one, so it extends to the offspring - i.e., the obligation to return to the Haram is established for the offspring as well; because permanent attributes in mothers extend to the offspring, like freedom, kitabah, and tadbir. If it is said: Liability for the offspring here is problematic considering [the case of] one who usurps a female gazelle, and she gives birth, and they die, where he is not liable for the offspring. The answer: Because the offspring in the case of the female gazelle of the Haram is a right of Allah, and He is the one demanding return, unlike in usurpation, where it is a right of the slave and he did not demand it - even if he demanded it and the usurper prevented him, he would be liable. And because the cause for liability in the case of the female gazelle of the Haram is removal of safety, and this existed in the offspring by extension, while in usurpation it is removal of the owner's hand, which did not exist because the offspring came into existence while with the usurper of its mother. Upon this, he is liable for the offspring of the female gazelle regardless of whether the one who took the mother was able to return it or not. (Ayni, Fath) He is not liable for the offspring because it is game of the Halal and the effect of his action was removed by atonement; because when he paid the penalty for the mother, she remained non-liable, so likewise the offspring. Therefore, the one who took it owns it - i.e., as odious ownership. Similarly, if [a female gazelle] increased after being taken out in body or price, then gave birth, and they die, he is liable for both; but if after paying the penalty, he is not liable for them. Similarly, if he sold it and it increased or gave birth in the hand of the buyer, then they die, the seller is liable before atonement, not after. (Ayni, Miskin)
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling regarding liability for game taken from the Haram, specifically a female gazelle. It begins by stating that if someone takes a female gazelle of the Haram and she gives birth, then both mother and offspring die, the taker is liable for both (i.e., must pay the penalty for both). However, if he pays the penalty for the mother first, and then she gives birth, he is not liable for the offspring because the offspring is considered game of the Halal after the mother's penalty has removed her sanctity. The passage then explains that any transaction involving such a gazelle (sale, gift, charity) is invalid. The commentator notes that if the gazelle is taken from the Haram, the taker must return and release it. If he fails to do so and she gives birth after release outside the Haram, he is liable for both. The reason given is that game taken from the Haram must be returned to its place of safety, and this attribute (of being required to be returned) extends to the offspring, similar to how attributes like freedom extend from mother to child. An objection is raised: in the case of usurpation (ghasp) of a female gazelle, if she gives birth and both die, the usurper is not liable for the offspring. The answer distinguishes the two cases: in the Haram case, it is a right of Allah to have the game returned, so liability arises from the removal of safety, which extends to the offspring. In usurpation, it is a right of the owner, and the owner did not demand the return of the offspring (since it was not owned). Also, the cause of liability in the Haram case is the removal of security (amān), which applies to the offspring derivatively, whereas in usurpation it is the removal of the owner's hand, which does not apply to the offspring since it was born in the usurper's hand. The passage concludes by stating that if the gazelle increases in value or body after being taken out, then gives birth, and both die, the taker is liable before atonement but not after. Similarly, if he sells it and it increases or gives birth in the buyer's hand, the seller is liable before atonement but not after.

Key Terms
ظبية الحرم (zabyat al-ḥaram): A female gazelle that is within the sacred precincts of the Haram of Mecca, thus protected and inviolable.
ضمن (ḍamina): Liability to pay compensation or penalty; here, it means the taker must pay the penalty (jaza') for the game.
جزاء (jazā'): The obligatory penalty for killing or wounding game while in ihram or in the Haram; usually a sacrificial animal or equivalent.
سراية الوصف (sariyat al-wasf): The transmission of a legal attribute from a mother to her offspring; here, the attribute of requiring return to the Haram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A female gazelle from the Haram and her offspring before atonement incur liability; after atonement, the offspring is halal and no penalty is due.

Passing the Miqat Without Ihram and Returning
Ruling on passing the miqat without ihram and the effect of returning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on passing the miqat without ihram and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement on whether returning with ihram and talbiyah drops the dam.

Core Idea
Passing the miqat without ihram incurs a dam, but returning as a muhrim may drop it—only with talbiyah according to Abu Hanifa.

In this lesson, we explore a practical scenario in Hajj: what happens if someone passes the miqat without entering into ihram? The Hanafi school holds that a dam (sacrificial animal) becomes wajib. However, there is a significant intra-madhhab dispute about whether returning to the miqat and entering ihram can nullify that obligation, and whether the recitation of talbiyah is a necessary condition. Understanding the different positions and their proofs is essential for both the pilgrim and the mufti.

Guided Path
1
State the base ruling
Identify the act: passing the miqat without ihram. The consequence: a dam becomes wajib. Note that this applies whether the person is a Makkan or not, and whether free or slave.
The dam is one animal for both Hajj and Umrah.
2
Identify the condition for dropping the dam
The text introduces the possibility of returning to the miqat as a muhrim. This can drop the dam. Determine the basic condition: returning and entering ihram before engaging in the actions of Hajj/Umrah.
If the pilgrim begins the actions (e.g., tawaf), consensus says the dam does not drop even if he returns later.
3
Note the internal disagreement on talbiyah
Abu Hanifa requires that the returning pilgrim recite talbiyah. The Two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that simply returning as a muhrim suffices, with or without talbiyah.
This difference stems from their understanding of what constitutes fulfilling the 'right of the miqat'.
4
Consider Zafar's dissenting view
Zafar argues that returning never drops the dam, analogizing to leaving Arafat early and returning after sunset, where the dam remains.
The Hanafi response distinguishes by noting that in the Arafat case the omission is not made up, whereas returning to the miqat directly makes up the omission.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that returning with ihram drops the dam. However, to be safe, one should also recite talbiyah due to Abu Hanifa's stronger proof regarding the original obligation (azima).
The zahir al-riwayah supports the Two's view that talbiyah is not required, but Abu Hanifa's view is considered his own madhhab and is preferred for caution.

Source Text

(فتح) غير محرم كان على المصنف أن يقول: من جاوز الميقات غير محرم لزمه دم، إلا أنه اكتفى بما فهم اقتضاء من قوله: "بطل الدم"؛ لأن مجاوزته بمنزلة إيجاب الإحرام على نفسه، فإذا جاوزه بلا إحرام لزمه دم واحد للنسكين إما حج أو عمرة لأن مجاوزة الميقات بنية دخول الحرم بمنزلة إيجاب الإحرام على نفسه، وكذلك إذا أوجب بالفعل، كما إذا افتتح صلاة التطوع ثم أفسدها وجب عليه قضاء ركعتين كما أوجبها بالقول، وإطلاق من جاوز يشمل المكي أيضاً، حتى أنه لو دخل الحل وأحرم بحجة لزمه دم، وكذا المتمتع لو أحرم بعمرة من الحرم؛ لأن ميقات المكي الحرم للحج والحل للعمرة، ولا فرق في لزوم الدم بسبب مجاوزة الميقات بلا إحرام بين الحر والعبد. (فتح) ثم عاد محرما : أي بحج أو عمرة، وسواء عاد إلى الميقات الذي تجاوزه أو عاد إلى غيره أقرب أو أبعد في ظاهر الرواية، وعن أبي يوسف إن كان الذي رجع إليه محاذياً لما فاته أو أبعد سقط وإلا لم يسقط الدم بالرجوع إليه، والصحيح ظاهر الرواية. (فتح) ملبياً: شرط التلبية إنما هو مذهب أبي حنيفة، أي بطل الدم عنده لو عاد محرماً ملبياً، وقالا: إن رجع إليه محرماً فليس عليه شيء لبى أو لم يلب، وقال زفر لا يسقط الدم؛ لأن الجناية لم يرتفع بالعود، فصار كما إذا أفاض عن عرفات قبل الإمام، ثم عاد إليها بعد الغروب، وبه قالت الثلاثة. ولنا: أنه تدارك المتروك في وقته إلا أن التدارك عندهما بما إذا عاد محرماً؛ لأنه أظهر حق الميقات، كما إذا مر به محرماً ساكتا، وعند أبي حنيفة التدارك بعوده محرماً ملبياً؛ لأن العزيمة في الإحرام من دويرة أهله، فإذا ترخص بالتأخير إلى الميقات وجب عليه قضاء حقه بإنشاء التلبية، وهذا بخلاف الإفاضة؛ لأنه لم يتدارك المتروك، ولو عاد بعد استلام الحجر لا يسقط الدم بالاتفاق، ولو عاد قبل الإحرام وأحرم منها يسقط الدم بالاتفاق، وهذا الخلاف فيما إذا رجع قبل أن يشتغل بأعمال ما عقد الإحرام له فلو شرع في الأفعال ثم عاد إلى الميقات لا يسقط الدم (عيني مستخلص) أصلاً. ثم أفسد: تلك العمرة أو الحج، ثم عاد إلى الميقات من عامه ذلك. (ط، ع) وقضى ما أفسده . بإحرام عند الميقات. (ط،ع) من الحج والعمرة
Chapter on passing the time without ihram – i.e., explaining its ruling. He meant by 'time' the miqat. One who passes the miqat without being in ihram, then returns as a muhrim with talbiyah, or passes then assumes ihram for umrah, then invalidates [it] and makes it up – i.e., while being to the miqat, the miqat without ihram inside the miqat or Hajj. [Commentary]: 'Chapter on passing etc.': When he finished explaining the offense in ihram and its rulings, he began explaining the offense without ihram due to the suitability between them. He only singled it out as an independent chapter because of the contrast between them. 'Time' linguistically is specific to time, and 'miqat' is common between time and place. Here 'time' is intended to mean the spatial miqat metaphorically, evidenced by 'passing'. (Fath) 'Without ihram' – it was upon the author to say: 'One who passes the miqat without ihram is obligated a dam', but he sufficed with what is understood by implication from his statement 'the dam is nullified'; because his passing is tantamount to imposing ihram upon himself, so if he passes it without ihram, one dam is incumbent for the two rites – either Hajj or Umrah – because passing the miqat with intention to enter the Haram is tantamount to imposing ihram upon himself. Similarly, if he imposes it by action, like if he begins a supererogatory prayer then invalidates it, he must make up two rak'ahs just as he obligated it by speech. The absolute phrase 'one who passes' includes the Makkan as well, so that if he enters the Halal and assumes ihram for Hajj, a dam is incumbent on him. Likewise the mutamatti' if he assumes ihram for Umrah from the Haram, because the miqat of the Makkan is the Haram for Hajj and the Halal for Umrah. There is no difference in the obligation of a dam due to passing the miqat without ihram between a free person and a slave. (Fath) 'Then he returned as a muhrim': i.e., for Hajj or Umrah, and it is the same whether he returns to the miqat he passed or to another nearer or farther, according to the apparent riwayah. On the authority of Abu Yusuf, if the one he returned to is parallel to what he missed or farther, it drops; otherwise, the dam does not drop by returning to it. The correct is the apparent riwayah. (Fath) 'With talbiyah': The condition of talbiyah is only the madhhab of Abu Hanifah – i.e., the dam becomes void with him if he returns as a muhrim with talbiyah. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: If he returns to it as a muhrim, nothing is upon him whether he said talbiyah or not. Zafar said: The dam does not drop because the offense is not removed by returning, so it is like one who leaves Arafat before the Imam, then returns to it after sunset. And with him said the three. Our proof: He has made up what was omitted in its time – except that, according to the two, the making up is by returning as a muhrim; because he manifested the right of the miqat, just like when one passes it as a muhrim silently. According to Abu Hanifah, the making up is by returning as a muhrim with talbiyah; because the original obligation (azima) is to assume ihram from his own dwelling, so if he took the concession (rukhsa) of delaying to the miqat, it is obligatory upon him to fulfill its right by initiating talbiyah. This is different from the ifada [from Arafat] because he did not make up what was omitted. And if he returns after touching the Black Stone, the dam does not drop by agreement. And if he returns before ihram and assumes ihram from there, the dam drops by agreement. This disagreement is when he returns before engaging in the actions for which ihram was contracted. If he begins the actions then returns to the miqat, the dam does not drop at all. (Ayni, extracted) 'Then he invalidated': that umrah or Hajj, then returned to the miqat in that same year. (T, A) 'And made up what he invalidated' with ihram at the miqat. (T, A) of Hajj and Umrah.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the chapter regarding one who passes the miqat without ihram. It begins by clarifying that 'time' here means the spatial miqat. The ruling: if one passes the miqat without ihram, he incurs a dam (one sacrificial animal) because passing the miqat with the intention to enter the Haram is equivalent to obligating ihram upon himself. This applies whether for Hajj or Umrah. The passage then discusses the situation where the person returns to the miqat and enters ihram. According to the apparent riwayah, returning to any miqat (nearer or farther) can drop the dam, but Abu Yusuf had a condition that it must be parallel or farther. The main dispute is about the requirement of talbiyah: Abu Hanifa holds that the dam is dropped only if he returns and says the talbiyah; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that returning as a muhrim without talbiyah suffices; Zafar holds that the dam never drops by returning, analogizing to one who leaves Arafat early and returns after sunset. The Hanafi response is that returning makes up for the omission in its time, but the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) consider the making up by simply returning as a muhrim, while Abu Hanifa requires talbiyah because the original obligation from one's home was to say talbiyah. The passage also clarifies that if he begins the actions of Hajj (like touching the Black Stone) and then returns, the dam does not drop by agreement. It concludes with a subcase: if he invalidated his ihram and then returned to the miqat and made it up, the ruling applies accordingly.

Key Terms
ميقات (mīqāt): The designated place (or time) for entering the state of ihram for Hajj or Umrah. Here it refers primarily to the spatial boundary.
دم (dam): A sacrificial animal (e.g., a sheep) that is wajib as compensation for certain violations during Hajj/Umrah.
تلبية (talbiyah): The phrase 'Labbayka Allahumma labbayk...' recited when entering ihram; considered an integral part of ihram by Abu Hanifa for the purpose of dropping the dam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is required for the dam to be dropped when one returns to the miqat after having passed it without ihram?
A) Simply returning as a muhrim.
B) Returning and reciting talbiyah.
C) Returning and performing two rak'ahs.
D) Returning and sacrificing an animal.

Key Takeaway
Passing the miqat without ihram obligates a dam, but returning as a muhrim before starting Hajj actions can drop it; the requirement of talbiyah is an intra-Hanafi dispute, with the safer practice being to recite it.

Entering Makkah Without Ihram and the Obligation of Hajj/Umrah per Entry
Ruling on an outsider entering Makkah without ihram for a need and the obligation of hajj/umrah for each entry

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on entering Mecca without ihram for a need and explain why each such entry obligates a separate hajj or umrah, with the exception of overlapping (tadakhul) for the last entry.

Core Idea
Every entry into Mecca without ihram obligates a separate hajj or umrah; only the most recent entry can be covered by a later ihram.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq addresses a critical distinction: an outsider (afaqi) who enters Mecca without ihram incurs an obligation, regardless of intention. It also clarifies the Hanafi position on multiple entries and the concept of tadakhul (overlapping). Understanding this helps you see how the Hanafi school balances the sanctity of the Haram with practical needs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key categories of people
Distinguish between an outsider (afaqi) who must enter ihram from the miqat and a resident of the miqat area (like the gardener of Bustan Bani Amir) who may enter without ihram for a need.
The term 'Kufi' in the text is a placeholder for any afaqi.
2
State the ruling when an outsider enters without ihram
An afaqi who enters Mecca even for a need (not intending hajj/umrah) commits a violation and becomes wajib to perform one of the two rituals.
This contradicts al-Shafi'i, who permits entry without ihram if no ritual is intended.
3
Analyze the obligation for multiple entries
Each separate entry without ihram creates a new wajib. The text explicitly states: 'من دخل مرة وجب عليه لكل مرة حجة أو عمرة' – a separate obligation for each entry.
This is a key practical consequence: multiple visits can lead to multiple debts.
4
Examine the overlapping (tadakhul) rule
If the outsider later returns to the miqat and enters ihram for hajj or umrah, it only covers the most recent entry. Earlier obligations remain as debts that require separate fulfillment.
The Hanafi majority (Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn) allow this via istihsan, while Zufar rejects it by qiyas.
5
Understand the proof for tadakhul
The obligation is to revere the Haram by coming with ihram. Making it up from the miqat compensates the deficiency for the last entry, similar to a vowed i'tikaf being fulfilled by Ramadan fasting.
The analogy highlights that the debt is discharged when the action is performed in its proper time and place.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلو دخل الكوفي البستان الحاجة له دخول مكة بلا إحرام، ووقته أي الآفاقي البستان، ومن دخل . أي جاز له بطل الدم: [جواب الشرط، أي سقط الدم الذي وجب عليه في المسئلتين. (ط، ع)] أي في هذه الصورة أيضاً، وهي إذا جاوز ثم أحرم بعمرة ثم أفسد العمرة وقضاها بإحرام من الميقات، وعند زفر: لا يسقط في هذه الصورة أيضاً؛ لأنه بالعود لا ترتفع الجناية، ولنا أنه تلافى المتروك بالقضاء من الميقات فانجبر ذلك النقصان. (فتح) فلو دخل الكوفي نبه بهذا التفريع على أن ما مر من لزوم الإحرام من الميقات إنما هو على من قصد أحد النسكين أو دخول مكة أو الحرم، فقصد مكة أو الحرم موجب له، سواء قصد نسكاً أو لا، أما إذا قصد مكاناً من الحل داخل الميقات، فإنه لا يجوز له الدخول لالتحاقه بأهله، سواء نوى الإقامة الشرعية أي خمسة عشر يوماً أو لم ينو، وعن أبي يوسف: إن نوى الإقامة أقل من خمسة عشر يوماً لا يكون منهم، فلا يكون له أن يدخل مكة بغير إحرام والحاصل أن من كان دون الميقات له دخول مكة بلا إحرام سواء قصد حاجة بالبستان عند دخوله أم لا، وليس المراد بالكوفي خصوصه، بل أراد به الآفاقي كوفياً كان أم لا، ولو أحرم من البستان للحج ولم يدخل مكة حتى وقف بعرفة أجزأه؛ لأنه أحرم من ميقاته ولم يترك نسكاً واجباً، فلا يلزمه شيء كأهل البستان. (عيني، فتح) البستان: أي بستان بني عامر، وهي قرية في داخل الميقات وخارج الحرم، تسمى الآن نخلة وعشرون ميلاً من مكة رمز الحقائق: ۱۸۲/۱] لحاجة عرضت له أي لا لدخول مكة ثم بدا له أن يدخل. (ط ، ع ) بلا إحرام لأنه التحق بأهل البستان، و للبستاني أن يدخل مكة بلا إحرام للحاجة، فكذا له عيني ووقته أي ميقاته إذا أراد الإحرام. (ع) البستان أراد جميع الحل الذي بينه وبين الحرم كالبستاني. [رمز الحقائق: ۱۸۲/۱] ومن دخل إلخ : أي يجب عليه أحد النسكين لكل دخول، ثم إذا في عامه ذلك حج صح من دخوله مكة، يعني من أخر دخوله بغير إحرام؛ فإنه لو دخلها مرارا وجب عليه لكل مرة حجة أو عمرة، فإذا خرج فأحرم بنسك أجزأه عن آخر دخوله لا عما له قبله؛ لأن الواجب قبل الإحرام صار دينا في ذمته فلا يسقط إلا بالنية، والغرض أنه إذا دخل كوفي مكة بلا إحرام لحاجة له يجب عليه عمرة أو حجة، إن كانت في وقتها، خلافا للشافعي بناء على أن له أن يدخل مكة بغير إحرام إن لم يرد أحد النسكين عنده وعندنا: ليس له ذلك، فإن رجع إلى الميقات فأهل بحجة الإسلام وعما لزمه بدخوله مكة بطريق التداخل، وفي القياس لا يجوز وهو قول زفر؛ لأنه بدخول وجب عليه حجة أو عمرة وصار ذلك دينا في ذمته، فلا يتأدى إلا بالنية ولنا أنه تلافي المتروك في وقته؛ لأن الواجب عليه تعظيم هذه البقعة بالإحرام كما إذا أتاها بحجة الإسلام في الابتداء، وصار كالاعتكاف المنذور؛ فإنه يتأدى بصوم رمضان. (مستخلص، فتح)
So if the Kufi enters the garden for a need, he has permission to enter Mecca without ihram, and his time (i.e., miqat) is the garden (i.e., the out-of-town person). And whoever enters... i.e., it is permissible for him. The blood is nullified: [Answer of condition, i.e., the blood that was obligatory in the two issues is dropped. (T, A)] i.e., also in this scenario: when he passes then enters ihram for umrah, then invalidates the umrah and makes it up with ihram from the miqat. According to Zufar, it is not dropped in this scenario either, because the violation is not removed by return. Our proof: he made up what was omitted by the makeup from the miqat, so that deficiency is compensated. (Fath) 'So if the Kufi enters' – he alerted by this subdivision that what was mentioned about the obligation of ihram from the miqat applies only to one who intends one of the two rituals or entering Mecca or the Haram. So intending Mecca or the Haram necessitates it, whether he intends a ritual or not. As for one who intends a place in the hill within the miqat, he is not permitted to enter (Mecca without ihram) because he is attached to its people, whether he intends the legal residence (i.e., fifteen days) or not. From Abu Yusuf: if he intends residence less than fifteen days, he is not from them, so he may not enter Mecca without ihram. The result: whoever is below the miqat may enter Mecca without ihram, whether he intends a need in the garden at his entry or not. The term 'Kufi' is not specific; rather he means the out-of-town person, Kufi or not. If he enters ihram from the garden for hajj and does not enter Mecca until he stands at Arafat, it suffices him, because he entered ihram from his miqat and did not omit an obligatory ritual, so nothing is due on him, like the people of the garden. (Ayni, Fath) 'al-Bustan' i.e., the garden of Bani Amir, a village inside the miqat and outside the Haram, now called Nakhla, twenty miles from Mecca. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1:182) For a need that occurred to him, i.e., not for entering Mecca, then he later decides to enter. (T, A) Without ihram because he is attached to the people of the garden, and the gardener may enter Mecca without ihram for a need; so by analogy, he may do so. (Ayni) And his time i.e., his miqat if he wants to enter ihram. (A) Al-Bustan means all the hill area between it and the Haram, like the gardener. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1:182) 'And whoever enters... etc.': i.e., one of the two rituals is obligatory for each entry. Then if in that same year he performs hajj, it is valid from his entering Mecca, meaning one who delayed his entry without ihram; if he enters it multiple times, a hajj or umrah is due for each time. If he leaves then enters ihram for a ritual, it suffices for his last entry, not for what preceded it, because the obligation before ihram became a debt in his liability and is not removed except by intention. The point: if a Kufi enters Mecca without ihram for a need, an umrah or hajj is obligatory upon him, if it is in its time, contrary to al-Shafi'i, based on his view that he may enter Mecca without ihram if he does not intend one of the rituals. According to us, he does not have that right. If he returns to the miqat and enters ihram for the hajj of Islam, it also suffices for what he owed by entering Mecca through overlapping. By qiyas, this is not permissible, and that is the view of Zufar, because by entering, a hajj or umrah became due and became a debt in his liability, so it cannot be performed except with intention. Our proof: he made up what was missed in its time, because what is obligatory is revering this place by ihram, as if he came with the hajj of Islam at the start. It is like a vowed i'tikaf: it can be performed by fasting in Ramadan. (Mustakhlas, Fath)
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage addresses a secondary ruling: an outsider (e.g., a Kufi) who enters Mecca without ihram for a need, and the obligation that arises from each such entry. The text begins by explaining that the previous discussion about the blood penalty applies to one who intends a ritual or entering Mecca/the Haram. However, if a person who lives within the miqat (like the 'people of the garden') enters Mecca without ihram, it is permissible for a need. The term 'Kufi' is generically used for any outsider (afaqi). The garden (Bustan Bani Amir) is a place inside the miqat but outside the Haram, so its residents are considered like those below the miqat: they may enter Mecca without ihram for a need. The main ruling: every time an outsider enters Mecca without ihram (whether for a need or not), he becomes obligated to perform either a hajj or an umrah. If he enters multiple times, each entry requires a separate ritual. However, he cannot perform one ritual (e.g., hajj) and make it count for all previous entries unless he intends that when entering ihram. If he returns to the miqat and enters ihram for hajj, it can cover the obligation of the last entry through overlapping (tadakhul), but not earlier entries. There is a disagreement with al-Shafi'i, who allows entering Mecca without ihram if one does not intend a ritual; the Hanafi school does not permit it. The proof is based on the principle that revering the sacred precinct requires ihram from the miqat; entering without it creates a debt. The analogy is made to a vowed i'tikaf that can be fulfilled by Ramadan fasting. This passage also clarifies that the obligation is per entry, and the compensation (qada) from the miqat remedies the deficiency.

Key Terms
آفاقي (āfāqī): A person from outside the miqat (the out-of-town person), as opposed to a resident of the Haram or the miqat area.
تداخل (tadākhul): Overlapping of obligations; here, one hajj/umrah can satisfy the obligation from multiple entries if performed after the last entry.
بستان بني عامر (bustān banī ʿāmir): A specific garden (village) inside the miqat but outside the Haram, whose residents may enter Mecca without ihram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens if an outsider enters Mecca without ihram multiple times in one year?
A) One hajj or umrah suffices for all entries
B) A separate hajj or umrah is due for each entry
C) He owes nothing if he intended no ritual
D) Only the first entry obligates a ritual

Key Takeaway
An outsider entering Mecca without ihram incurs a wajib hajj/umrah per entry, and overlapping (tadākhul) only covers the most recent entry if ihram is from the miqat.
